Integrating Christian Faith and Social Work: Reflections of a Social Work Educator

Author: Sherwood, David A11 Roberts Wesleyan Coll, Rochester, NY sherwood@roberts.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61626503?accountid=14667

Abstract: All teachers & practitioners of social work, Christian or not, integrate belief systems into their understanding, teaching, & practice. Perceptions & understandings of anything are always situated in historical, cultural, & personal stories. Facts are always interpreted on the basis of beliefs & values. Worldviews are always in a hermeneutical interaction with one's experience of the world, asking four questions: Where are we? Who are we? What's wrong? What's the remedy? These questions are useful ones to use to "interrogate" the assumptions & implications of all values, theories, & methods. A Christian worldview supports the mission of social work & its Code of Ethics while simultaneously informing & critiquing it. Other worldviews may profitably inform & critique current Christian understandings also as part of the hermeneutical spiral. Since human understanding is always limited, there will always be conscientious disagreement at the practice level. Since beliefs inevitably affect practice, one must undertake integration intentionally, not accidentally, with integrity & responsibility. 17 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Idea of Social Work: Back to the Future

Author: Kuhlmann, Edward G11 Social Work Dept, Eastern Coll, Saint Davids, PA kuhlmann@eastern.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61669827?accountid=14667

Abstract: As the profession of social work marks its centennial & the North American Assoc of Christians in Social Work approaches its semi-centennial, three trends in social work indicating that the profession has gone astray from its vital historic mission are addressed: (1) the distortion of individualism, (2) the distraction of private practice, & (3) the temptation of profit. The essentially social nature & focus of social work practice is established on the basis of human sociality & the relationship between private personal troubles & public issues, communities, & structures. Social workers, in general, & Christian social workers, in particular, are challenged to rediscover why society created a social work profession in the first place, to regain the moral authority it historically has had by working to maximize human well-being by mediating interaction between those who are least able to provide for & protect themselves & the social systems that affect them. 13 References. Adapted from the source document.

Graduate Social Work Students' Attitudes and Behaviors toward Spirituality and Religion: Issues for Education and Practice

Author: Kaplan, Alicia J1; Dziegielewski, Sophia F1 c/o Dziegielewski -- School Social Work, U Central Florida, Orlando

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61669883?accountid=14667

Abstract: Examines attitudes of Master of Social Work students about spirituality & religion & the degree to which these attitudes are incorporated into social work practice, drawing on survey data from 84 second-year graduate social work students from a southeastern US university. Most graduate students stated that they value the role of spirituality & religion in their personal & professional lives. Significant relationships were found between the degree of valuing spirituality & childhood religious ties, as well as valuing spirituality & the level of active participation in religious activities. These students, however, did not indicate that they were able to integrate spiritual & religious concepts in their work with clients, nor were they comfortable in doing so. Implications for social work practice & education are discussed, including the reported lack of adequate training & preparation during graduate education to deal with this issue. 1 Table, 14 References. Adapted from the source document.

Abstract: Examines the relation of the concept of social welfare work in the Lutheran Church to its history, eg, Martin Luther's contributions, the pietistic influence, the Inner Mission movement, & the roles of immigration & geographical settlement, on the formative years of social welfare history in the US. 29 References. When Professional Ethics and Religious Politics Conflict: A Case Study

Author: Garland, Diana R11 Division Social Work, Baylor U, Waco, TX diana_garland@baylor.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61654397?accountid=14667

Abstract: Recounts the events surrounding the firing of the Dean of the Carver School of Church Social Work of the Southern Baptist Theological Seminary. These events are told from the subjective perspective of the ousted dean. The reader is invited to consider & critique the author's account as a case study of the ethical dilemmas social workers sometimes confront in administration, eg, the administration of religious institutions. 4 References. Adapted from the source document.

Pluralism, Tolerance and Respect for Diversity: Engaging Our Deepest Differences within the Bond of Civility

Author: Sherwood, David A11 Roberts Wesleyan Coll, Rochester, NY sherwood@roberts.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61659073?accountid=14667

Abstract: Appropriate tolerance for others & respect for diversity are more complex & difficult to achieve than the rhetoric of either relativism or absolutism. Despite the rhetoric, few, if any, social workers are relativists. At some point social workers find themselves confronting something other than diversity, something they believe to be "wrong" & morally obligated to oppose. Moral rules, whether from the Code of Ethics or the Ten Commandments, can guide social workers in their moral reflection, but they cannot give absolute prescriptions when they conflict with each other in practice. Real tolerance & respect for diversity requires honest recognition of differences & civility in the exploration of their meaning. 3 References. Adapted fronm the source document.

What Do We Owe the Elderly? Thinking Christianly about Rationing Health Care Resources

Author: Brandsen, Cheryl Kreykes11 Dept Sociology/Social Work/Criminal Justice, Calvin Coll brac@calvin.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61621770?accountid=14667

Abstract: Considers how policy practitioners might respond to inevitable health care rationing concerns among the elderly. Since any form of rationing results in people going without beneficial services, Christian social workers who make & implement such policy decisions will need a deep & thoughtful Christian & social work ethic to guide their work. Implications of stewardship in a community of image-bearers involving both love & justice are explored. An understanding of being made in God's image moves Christian social workers toward affirming egalitarian conceptions of justice, within a context of democratic deliberation, as being congruent with their calling to image God as policy practitioners. 66 References. Adapted fronm the source document.

Perceptions of Conflict between Christianity and Social Work: A Preliminary Study

Author: Pellebon, Dwain A11 School Social Work, U Oklahoma, Norman dpellebon@ou.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61621823?accountid=14667

Abstract: To measure perceptions of social workers & the social work profession, questionnaire data from 145 members of nondenominational churches are drawn on to examine two dependent variables: (1) perceptions of conflict between Christianity & the social work profession & (2) the degree of agreement with social work values. Statistically significant findings indicate that this sample both perceives a high degree of conflict with social workers & disagrees with humanistic interpretations of social work values. Highlighted is the importance of social workers engaging in spiritually & religiously sensitive exploration of clients' perceptions to understand them & to help the clients understand social workers & their roles. 2 Tables, 25 References. Adapted fronm the source document.

A Forgiving State of Heart: Narrative Reflections on Social Work Practice from a Christian Perspective

Author: Graham, John R1; Bradshaw, Cathryn1 Faculty Social Work, U Calgary, Alberta jgraham@ucalgary.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61648057?accountid=14667

Abstract: The authors, both social workers who conceive of forgiveness in relation to their Christian formation, share their experiences. Three practice case examples illuminate the inextricable links between the theological & social work practice applications of giving & receiving forgiveness through vulnerability, the unguarded heart, & release. Coming to forgiveness is a grief process -- a process of remembering, experiencing & owning the emotions, choosing to acknowledge the pain, & releasing one's self & the other. This reflection is anchored by such mystics/thinkers as D. Bonhoeffer (1986), R. Niebuhr (1944), P. Tillich (1952), & L. Van Der Post (1966). 11 References. Adapted fronm the source document.

Religious Discrimination in Social Work: An International Survey of Christian Social Workers

Author: Ressler, Lawrence E1; Hodge, David R1 Roberts Wesleyan Coll, Rochester, NY ressler@roberts.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61666437?accountid=14667

Abstract: Explores perceptions of religious discrimination in social work, using survey data from 172 members of the North American Assoc of Christians in Social Work. Quantitative results indicated that a majority of respondents (53%) had personally experienced religious discrimination, with concern being expressed that the social work profession was not complying with the six standards in the Code of Ethics that directly & indirectly address religion. Although qualitative analysis revealed that institutions of higher learning constituted the most prominent setting in which discrimination occurred, the results indicated systemic discrimination throughout the profession. As hypothesized, a general pattern emerged whereby the more conservative the theological orientation of the respondent, the greater the degree of religious discrimination reported. It is suggested that the profession may be on the verge of abandoning its ethically mandated stance of inclusion & diversity & adopting a posture of overt oppression directed toward Christians & other people of faith. 53 References. Adapted fronm the source document.

Maximizing the Contribution of Faith-Based Organizations to Solve Today's Most Urgent Social Problems

Author: Sider, Ronald J11 Eastern Baptist Theological Seminary, Wynnewood, PA ronsider@esa-online.org

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61666478?accountid=14667

Abstract: A remarkable historic window of opportunity exists for Christians, especially those in social work, to provide leadership in addressing problems of poverty through the development of faith-based organizations (FBOs). To seize this opportunity, strategic & timely work is needed in five areas: (1) capacity building to increase & strengthen holistic faith-based organizations addressing social problems; (2) education of the media, the universities, business, & government to have a clear understanding of the unique character & contribution of FBOs; (3) analysis & evaluation of holistic FBOs regarding their efficacy & key components; (4) increased funding via individual churches, foundations, & government; & (5) public policy reconceptualizaton. This call to action sketches some of the activities needed in each of these areas. 8 References. Adapted fronm the source document.

Tracking Charitable Choice: A Study of the Collaboration between Faith-Based Organizations and the Government in Providing Social Services in Nine States

Author: Sherman, Amy L11 Welfare Policy Center, Hudson Instit

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61505186?accountid=14667

Abstract: "Charitable Choice" provisions of 1996 federal welfare reform legislation attempted to make a way for faith-based organizations to participate in bidding for contracts to provide governmentally funded social welfare services without having to abandon their religious character & while maintaining strong safeguards for the religious liberty of recipients of services. This article is an excerpt from a major study supported by the Center for Public Justice of the implementation of Charitable Choice in nine states (CA, IL, MA, MI, MS, NY, WI, TX, & VA). The impact of Charitable Choice has been modest, but notable. Research found 84 new financial collaboratives since 1996 & 41 new nonfinancial relationships. Only two states, TX & WI, have codified Charitable Choice's detailed guidelines into formal contracts. Relatively few people either in government or in the faith community are knowledgeable about Charitable Choice. In all states, much remains to be done to bring government administrative procedures & procurement policies into harmony with the letter & spirit of the legislation. Churches & faith-based groups must learn more about the possibilities contained in Charitable Choice funding as well as clearly discern & articulate their missions in order to make informed decisions regarding participation in collaboration. Adapted from the source document.

Megachurch Involvement in Community Social Ministry: Extent and Effects in Three Congregations

Author: Thornburgh, Georgianne1; Wolfer, Terry A1 Lutheran Hospice, Mount Pleasant, SC gthornburgh@viperlink.net

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61503120?accountid=14667

Abstract: Recent decades have seen the emergence of megachurches, congregations with a regular attendance of more than 2,000. Because of both their pastoral emphasis & unusual size, megachurches may appear as a unique resource in the current context of welfare reform. This article reports a small study exploring the underlying mission & philosophy of megachurches; how these are transformed into service activities; & the impact of these activities upon the churches, their members, & local communities. It is based on in-depth interviews with leaders of three megachurches located in SC. This study suggests the importance of linking service activities with congregational mission & philosophy, & highlights the role of leaders in initiating, interpreting, & organizing service activities. It also identifies basic effects of these service activities. 19 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Spiritually Committed: An Examination of the Staff at Faith-Based Substance Abuse Providers

Author: Hodge, David R11 Washington U, Saint Louis, MO drh1@gwbssw.wustl.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61503200?accountid=14667

Abstract: Although faith-based providers are significant players in human service delivery & demonstrate high success rates, & global trends suggest increased utilization of their services, surprisingly little research has been done on provider staff. Accordingly, this paper examines staff characteristics (N = 32) at two faith-based AOD (alcohol & other drugs) residential care facilities. The typical staff member is young, well educated, raised in a family where religious involvement was emphasized, & motivated by an intrinsic spirituality. In what many would consider a counterintuitive finding, this study found little support for charitable choice, in spite of the presumed benefits, both materially & legally, such legislation would presumably provide staff. 3 Tables, 53 References. Adapted from the source document.

Volunteer Mentors in Social Welfare: Who They Are and What Happens When They Mentor

Author: Jonker, Patrice Hudson1; Koopman, Douglas L1 Gerontology Network Grand Rapids, MI

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61458197?accountid=14667

Abstract: Since 1996, MI's Family Independence Agency (FIA) has conducted pilot welfare reform projects built on newly emphasized themes of personal responsibility & community involvement. These projects are generically defined as "Project Zero" pilot projects because the state's goal is to reduce to zero the number of targeted AFDC households without earned income. In some pilots, the county FIA has turned to faith-based organizations to create mentor groups & make them available to clients. Faith-based mentoring may more effectively meet the complex range of client needs in gaining independence from welfare. Recruiting & motivating mentors through faith-based organizations may be more efficient because persons of faith have high motivation & commitment & a wide range of resources, including spiritual, to provide clients. However, troubling constitutional questions of church-state relations arise in the program itself. Mentors may change their religious, social & political views in ways that impede future mentoring efforts. This study presents results from two questionnaires sent to all Kent County mentors, investigating a range of demographic, behavioral, & attitudinal factors & the effects of the mentoring experience upon some of those factors. 3 Tables, 30 References. Adapted from the source document.

Between Imprisonment and Integrity: Rural Churches Respond to Poverty and Policy

Author: Amato-von Hemert, Katherine11 Lexington Theological Seminary, KY kavh2@mindspring.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61503251?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article reports findings from investigations of what rural church leaders think about the new welfare law, antipoverty service programs they offer their impoverished communities, & narrative that themes emerge from discussion of poverty policy. Given the Personal Responsibility & Work Opportunity Reconciliation Act of 1996 (PRWORA) "Charitable Choice" provisions, which authorize states to contract with religious congregations, church groups may become more significantly involved in social service provision. Ethnographic study with two African American churches in rural GA & two Caucasian churches in rural CO reveal widespread & strong agreement with PRWORA's treatment of time-limited cash assistance, teen pregnancy rates, & work requirements. Attitudes regarding appropriate church & government roles in serving impoverished people & the programs these churches operate are also analyzed. Themes of "imprisonment" & "integrity" appear as frameworks underlying poverty policy attitudes, told through stories of slave quarters, cemeteries, funeral homes, school bus snow tragedies, & public restrooms. Future poverty policy development would be enhanced by attention to these themes. 42 References. Adapted from the source document.

Religious and Spiritual Aspects of Human Practice

Author: Staral, Janice M; Staral, Janice M

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61458266?accountid=14667

The Faith Dimensions of Family Life

Author: Garland, Diana R11 School Social Work, Baylor U Waco, TX Diana_Garland@baylor.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61469849?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper reports a qualitative research project designed to explore the existence & nature of faith as a systemic characteristic of families. It assumes that families are primary environments in which children & adults develop & shape their understanding of life's meaning & purposes. It offers a beginning attempt to define how the narratives of family life reveal these processes of individual formation. Beyond individual formation, it looks for family narratives that indicate that faith may be a systemic characteristic of family systems themselves. 1 Appendix, 45 References. Adapted from the source document.

Religious Affiliation and Help Seeking: Does Denominational Membership Make a Difference?

Author: Watson, Jo-Ellen11 Seattle Pacific U, WA jewatson@spu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61505574?accountid=14667

Abstract: This study explores how denominational membership influences the help-seeking process of a battered woman church member. The study uses a quantitative methodology to determine whether women members of a mainline Protestant denomination (n = 211) differ from women members of a conservative Protestant denomination (N = 211), in which they would be willing to refer a battered woman church member for help. A model for understanding denominational differences is offered as well as empirical evidence that the differences were present in the two denomination samples used in this study. While members from both denominations differed less in their willingness to refer a battered woman to professional Christian services, there were significant differences in willingness to refer to nonreligious community-based services. Women members from a conservative denomination were less willing to refer to nonreligious community-based programs, some of which specialize in services for battered women, than women from the mainline denomination. Implications for the delivery of social services generally, & specifically those pertaining to battered Christian women, are explored. 4 Tables, 20 References. Adapted from the source document.

Alcohol Addiction and Social Work Practice: A Holistic Christian Paradigm

Author: Henning, Nelson L1; Firmin, Michael1 Cedarville Coll, OH henningn@Cedarville.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61467228?accountid=14667

Abstract: The Christian who practices social work can be uniquely qualified to provide services to individuals who are struggling with alcohol addiction. This paper supports the concept that there is a holistic paradigm social workers can use in acquiring a broad understanding of alcohol addiction. An overview of contemporary theories associated with alcohol abuse is presented along with a biblical means of understanding alcohol addiction. Implications for social work practice within a Christian context are discussed. 27 References. Adapted from the source document.

Culturally-Based Korean American Family Conflict and How Churches Can Help: An Exploratory Survey

Author: Moon, Sung Seek1; Wolfer, Terry A; Robinson, Margaret M1 School Social Work, U Georgia, Athens

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61475115?accountid=14667

Abstract: In recent decades, the Korean American population grew rapidly, primarily through immigration. As a result, many families experience the stress of acculturation as they adjust to American society. This study explored the primary sources of culturally based conflict between parents & children & their preferred sources of help with this conflict. Because churches have traditionally played a central role in the lives of Korean American immigrant families, the study also explored family members' attitudes toward the church & types of church programs they believe would help resolve culturally based conflict between parents & children. A convenience sample (N = 197) of parents & children was obtained from 15 Protestant Korean American congregations located in southeastern US. Given the sample's church involvement, the culturally based conflict identified by family members poses unique opportunities for church-based interventions. 3 Tables, 26 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Culturally-Based+Korean+American+Family+Conflict+and+How+Churches+Can+Help%3A+An+Exploratory+Survey&amp;rft.au=Moon%2C+Sung+Seek%3BWolfer%2C+Terry+A%3BRobinson%2C+Margaret+M&amp;rft.aulast=Moon&amp;rft.aufirst=Sung&amp;rft.date=2001-10-01&amp;rft.volume=28&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=106&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Gay and Lesbian Seventh-Day Adventists: Strategies and Outcomes of Resisting Homosexuality

Author: Drumm, Rene D11 Social Work Dept, Andrews U, Berrien Springs, MI Rdrumm@Andrews.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61442253?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article present finding from a qualitative study of 37 gay & lesbian Seventh-day Adventists. Using in-depth interviewing, the research explored participants' accounts for the home environments & examined participants' journeys in trying to reconcile a lesbian or gay sexual orientation with an identity as Christian. Findings offer contextual information regarding the participants' experience growing up in Adventist families & Adventist churches whose religious beliefs prohibited homosexual behavior. Finding highlight various strategies participants used to resist homosexuality, including several change strategies that appear unique to Christian gay & lesbian persons that have not been previously examined in the literature, & the outcomes of those efforts. These finding suggest implications for social work practitioners, social work educators, & faith-based communities. 24 References. Adapted from the source document.

Skilled Mental Health Helping: Perceived Capability and the Use of Referrals among U.S. Catholic Priests

Author: Kane, Michael N11 School Social Work, Florida Atlantic U, Boca Raton, FL MNKane@aol.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61442260?accountid=14667

Abstract: The Catholic Church is four times larger than the second largest Christian denomination in the US & operates the largest private network of social service agencies. As the Bush administration proposes increased involvement of faith-based organizations in service delivery, & because clergy are referral sources as well as direct providers of help, this study investigated the perceptions of Catholic priests regarding their competency to provide skilled help & their use of referrals to mental health professional. The findings suggest that clergy believe they have a clear sense of when people with problems should be referred to mental health professionals & regularly refer to those professionals. Most feel that they are capable of dealing with people's personal problems although most also agree that they have not been trained to recognize mental & emotional illnesses. Years of pastoral experience & age may be critical factors in these perceptions. Implications are discussed. 4 Tables, 27 References. Adapted from the source document.

____________________________________________________________ 

Ethical Integration of Faith and Social Work Practice: Evangelism

Author: Sherwood, David A11 Baylor U, Waco, TX David_Sherwood@baylor.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61481405?accountid=14667

Abstract: Christians in social work wonder how evangelism relates to their practice of social work; clients often fear that social workers will impose their beliefs on clients. This article explores the ethical application of Christian & social work values, ethics, & practice principles to working with clients in ways that maintain integrity for both clients & social workers. All social workers, Christian or not, have to develop competence in the handling of their own beliefs & values while working ethically & respectfully with clients. The article explores use & limits of the Code of Ethics (& the Bible), using the concept of a "Principle/Practice Pyramid" & arguing that ethical judgments will always be required because legitimate values sometimes come into conflict. Particular issues such as self-determination, informed consent, agency auspice, social work role, & client expectations are explored. Addressing spiritual & religious issues with clients is an appropriate dimension of competent, holistic practice, but engaging in direct evangelism in a professional relationship is almost never ethical because it usually involves the risk of exploitation of a vulnerable relationship without the integrity of informed consent. 4 References. Adapted from the source document.

Reflections on the Meaning of Sectarian, Religiously-Affiliated, and Faith-Based Language: Implications for Human Service Consumers

Author: Netting, F Ellen11 School Social Work, Virginia Commonwealth U, Richmond enetting@mail1.vcu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61508812?accountid=14667

Abstract: In this article, the author reflects on the changing terminology used to describe what are now called faith-related or faith-based organizations. Using a personal story of her Grandmother's journey in later life, questions are posed about the meaning of a retirement community's being faith-based for consumers of service. The importance of seeing language & symbols of faith as conveying different meanings is emphasized in the context of marketing to older persons. The lecture ends with the question: "Will we be vigilant in doing no harm in the name of faith?". 17 References. Adapted from the source document.

Thank You for Asking: The Experiences of Latina Immigrant Professional Women

Author: Nuesch-Olver, Delia11 Seattle Pacific U, WA denuol@spu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61526127?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article is based on an ethnographic study of professional women from Latin America who relocated to the US when their husbands received a work-related transfer within a religious organization. All the women have made separate transitions into American life from the male informants. When given the opportunity to share their experiences individually, the women speak of their loss of identity, the complications related to the non-transferal of their professional credentials, their personal struggles with their change of status, as well as of other tensions related to the lack of support systems. The appropriate responses from Christian social workers & from those they may help train in churches & Christian organizations could result in creative ways to support these people in their adjustment to life in the US. The final section begins a dialogue on how this challenge can be addressed. 8 References. Adapted from the source document.

When Religion and Sexual Orientation Collide: Ethical Dilemmas in Curriculum Standards for Social Work Education

Author: Vanderwoerd, Jim R11 Dordt Coll, Sioux Center, IA jwoerd@dordt.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61484025?accountid=14667

Abstract: The approval process of the Educational Policy & Accreditation Standards by the Council on Social Work Education completed in June 2001 brought to the surface recurring ethical dilemmas for the social work profession about how to accommodate the claims of different minority groups, in particular, religious minorities & those who are gay & lesbian. This paper summarizes these ethical dilemmas & argues that the concept of pluralism provides a framework that creates space within the profession for the respectful recognition of differences. 32 References. Adapted from the source document.

Author: Dalberg, David11 Salvation Army

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61487732?accountid=14667

Helping Those Victimized by the Terror of War: Reflections on the Dismantling of Cluster Bombs in a Lao Village

Author: Peachey, Titus11 Mennonite Central Committee, Akron, PA Titus_M_Peachy@mail.mcc.org

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61487758?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article describes the ongoing terror experienced by Lao villagers as a result of the massive bombing that took place during the Vietnam War. The author, who has personally been involved in the effort to destroy an estimated 9 million bomblets that remain unexploded in the ground, describes the effect the bombs still have on the Laotian people more than 30 years after the bombing stopped. 1 Reference. "Who Is Your Family?" Membership Composition of American Protestant Families

Author: Garland, Diana R11 School Social Work, Baylor U, Waco, TX Diana_Garland@baylor.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61533438?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article reports findings of a research study of the family life of active members in 32 Protestant congregations. Both survey data & family interviews were used to determine how families in these congregations define their membership. The author draws implications for how social workers in community agencies that work with Christian families, as well as congregational leaders, assess family membership & potential resources & strengths. 1 Table, 8 References. Meeting the Mental Health Needs of Christians in a Rural Maine County: A Survey of Clergy and Their Congregations

Author: Paul, Penney1; Hussey, Travis; Arnsberger, Pamela1 Care Development Maine, Presque Isle penney@copper.net

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61448131?accountid=14667

Abstract: Ten clergy & nine congregations participated in telephone & written anonymous surveys that addressed the perception, demand, & satisfaction of the Christian community in a large rural county regarding mental health services & needs. Specifically the rates of referrals, accessing patterns, services preferences, & comfort level of clergy & parishioners regarding mental health issues were examined & the possible need for a Christian counseling center assessed. In comparison of services expected from a Christian counseling center by clergy & parishioners, both clergy & congregational members identified the areas of family support, crisis services, children's services, & parenting. However, differences in expectations between clergy & their congregations exist in how mental health, substance abuse & residential services, & spiritual growth & development, & discipleship needs should be met. 5 Tables, 24 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Meeting+the+Mental+Health+Needs+of+Christians+in+a+Rural+Maine+County%3A+A+Survey+of+Clergy+and+Their+Congregations&amp;rft.au=Paul%2C+Penney%3BHussey%2C+Travis%3BArnsberger%2C+Pamela&amp;rft.aulast=Paul&amp;rft.aufirst=Penney&amp;rft.date=2002-10-01&amp;rft.volume=29&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.spage=224&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Reflections on Henri Nouwen's "Wounded Healer" as a Model for Contemporary Social Work Practice

Author: Allen, James D, Jr11 Worden School Social Work, Our Lady of the Lake U, San Antonio, TX allej@lake.ollusa.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61501325?accountid=14667

Abstract: Henri Nouwen presents the "wounded healer" as a model for contemporary ministry. This model of ministry requires moving away from approaches emphasizing professional distance to approaches that bring the minister into intimate contact with those seeking his/her assistance. Like the minister, the professional social worker is called into more intimate relationships with those he/she serves. Some key elements of this model of ministry are discussed. Implications for social work practice & education are identified. 5 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Reflections+on+Henri+Nouwen%27s+%22Wounded+Healer%22+as+a+Model+for+Contemporary+Social+Work+Practice&amp;rft.au=Allen%2C+James+D%2C+Jr&amp;rft.aulast=Allen&amp;rft.aufirst=James&amp;rft.date=2002-10-01&amp;rft.volume=29&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.spage=240&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Churches as Contexts for Social Work Practice: Connecting with the Mission and Identity of Congregations

Author: Sherwood, David11 Baylor U, Waco, TX David_Sherwood@baylor.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61548419?accountid=14667

Abstract: A small but increasing number of social workers are practicing social work in the context of local congregations, either as staff members or as faith-motivated volunteers. This article reflects on some characteristics of churches as contexts for practice & some of the issues that social workers may face as they connect their work with the identity & mission of the church & the nature of working with faith-motivated volunteers. Faith-motivated volunteers engage in social services in part because: (1) it is something that they believe "ought" to be done; (2) it is something that they believe fits their personal capacities, gifts, or "calling"; & (3) it is something that gives them experiences that lead to persistence in the activity. As contexts for practice, congregations: (A) are "host" settings; (B) are social communities; (C) sometimes spin off programs & services to their societal context; (D) may become advocates; (E) are voluntary organizations; (F) are mission-driven organizations; & (G) are cultural groups. Issues for social workers include: (a) professional role & identity; (b) competent handling of personal beliefs, values, & social work ethics; (c) confidentiality & multiple relationships; (d) "good-enough" fit; & (e) pastoral/prophetic role. 16 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Churches+as+Contexts+for+Social+Work+Practice%3A+Connecting+with+the+Mission+and+Identity+of+Congregations&amp;rft.au=Sherwood%2C+David&amp;rft.aulast=Sherwood&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft.date=2003-04-01&amp;rft.volume=30&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.spage=1&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Enough about Leveling the Playing Field: What's the Playing Field?

Author: Wubbenhorst, William1; Voll, Michelle N1 Faith-Based Programs, ORC Macro, Calverton, MD

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61429806?accountid=14667

Abstract: Church-state controversies have focused on the role of faith-based & community organizations in publicly funded human social service delivery. Neither side has sufficiently examined the terms of engagement by which financial & nonfinancial collaborations between government & nongovernmental organizations occur. The White House Office of Faith-Based & Community Initiatives first used the expression "unlevel playing field" when it documented in its first audit report (2001) the barriers that small faith-based & community organizations face in the procurement of services. The authors of this article argue that we need to reframe the debate by asking "What is the playing field?" Many of the barriers identified by the federal government are inherent to the design of the playing field & the most common set of rules used in social service procurement. The authors examine (1) the larger context of privatization & present 10 privatization approaches to social service funding; (2) feature a continuum with respect to the degree of government financing & control; (3) present a new framework for evaluating the relationship between government, providers, clients, & communities; & (4) end with a brief set of recommendations for public officials, policy researchers, & community leaders. 3 Tables, 2 Figures, 14 References. Adapted from the source document.

Introducing Ignatian Spirituality: Linking Self-Reflection with Social Work Ethics

Author: Staral, Janice M11 Dept Social & Cultural Sciences, Marquette U, Milwaukee, WI Janice.staral@marquette.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61496235?accountid=14667

Abstract: Ethical issues & dilemmas are a concern for all social workers. Solid ethical decision making requires the ability to reflect on available options & determine the best choice. The use of self-reflection can also be used as a self-care tool. This author provides an introduction to Ignatian Spirituality, which is central to Catholic, Jesuit universities, & links it to social work ethics. A key principle of Ignatian Spirituality is the importance of "reflecting on daily life," which can be implemented through a specific prayer form called the Examen of Conscience. The author explains how this method of reflection is used in the context of a social work ethics class & invites others to explore its potential. 33 References. Adapted from the source document.

Stable Vision: Charitable Choice and the Routinization of Charisma

Author: Schwartz, Ann11 Concordia U, Austin, TX schwartza@concordia.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/60462600?accountid=14667

Abstract: As Charitable Choice provisions of the 1996 Personal Responsibility & Work Opportunity Reconciliation Act promote wider government funding of faith-based organizations, those connected with such organizations express concerns that government involvement will corrupt the vision of religious leaders. Responding to these concerns, this essay examines their validity by drawing on Max Weber's understanding of charismatic leadership & the routinization of charisma in organizations as well as critical responses to his work. The conclusion from this theoretical analysis is that outwardly religious signs & practices serve to preserve charisma within institutions over time. As a result, it is suggested that the religious identity protections built into the Charitable Choice provisions may prevent religious leaders from becoming bureaucrats lacking the vision to motivate followers & change human lives. 24 References. Adapted from the source document.

Should Faith-Based Organizations Apply for Government Funds? The Potential Impact of Giving and Volunteering

Author: Horne, Christopher11 Georgia Instit Technology/Georgia State U, Atlanta chorne@gsu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61502353?accountid=14667

Abstract: With increased access to government funding opportunities, more administrators of faith-based organizations (FBOs) are facing the decision of whether or not to apply for government funds. One important consideration is the potential impact of receiving government funding on levels of private financial & volunteer support. In the absence of any data on FBO supporters' actual responses to government funding, this study employs a public opinion survey approach to assist FBO administrators' decision making. A survey of 786 Georgians revealed widespread support for government funding of FBOs, particularly among respondents who give to or volunteer for FBOs. Of these FBO supporters, approximately three-fourths say their giving & volunteering would not change if FBOs received government funds; of those whose giving & volunteering would change, about two-thirds say they would increase their support. While this study does not provide definitive guidance to FBO administrators facing this complex decision, the data do not support reluctance to apply for government funds for fear of weakening private support. 1 Table, 1 Appendix, 9 References. Adapted from the source document.

Religious Theories of Personality and Psychotherapy: East Meets West

Author: Harris, Helen Wilson; Harris, Helen Wilson

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61543103?accountid=14667

Decision Cases for Christians in Social Work: Introduction to the Special Issue

Author: Wolfer, Terry A11 Coll Social Work, U South Carolina, Columbia terry.wolfer@sc.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61430085?accountid=14667

Abstract: This special issue of Social Work & Christianity provides a set of decision cases involving religion, spirituality, & faith in social work practice. This introduction will briefly (1) describe how these cases differ from other types of cases, (2) outline a rationale for their use in the social work classroom, (3) provide background on the case writing process, (4) summarize the five cases included here, (5) suggest how to read the cases, (6) suggest how to teach the cases (& identify some additional resources), & (7) invite readers to interact further with the cases. 20 References. Is the Case Method Bad for Your Ethics? Exploring Ethical, Theoretical, and Factual Justifications for Practice

Author: Sherwood, David11 Baylor U, Waco, TX david_sherwood@baylor.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61449424?accountid=14667

Abstract: Some have raised the question of whether or not the use of open-ended cases to think about practice & ethical decision making might tend to legitimize consideration of inappropriate issues & options & lead to relativism in values & practice. This article argues that it is often necessary to consider a wide range of options but that this does not necessarily legitimize them, that practice frequently requires prioritization of competing values but that this should be grounded in ethical principles, & that the use of open-ended cases can be one of the best ways of exploring the ethical, theoretical, & factual justifications for practice understanding & action. A "Principle/Practice Pyramid" is outlined, it is noted that case study is no substitute for character development for those who wish to practice ethically. 5 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Is+the+Case+Method+Bad+for+Your+Ethics%3F+Exploring+Ethical%2C+Theoretical%2C+and+Factual+Justifications+for+Practice&amp;rft.au=Sherwood%2C+David&amp;rft.aulast=Sherwood&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft.date=2003-07-01&amp;rft.volume=30&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=117&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Preparing Social Work Students for Practice with Religious Congregations within the Context of Charitable Choice: The Grace House Ministry (A)

Author: Sherr, Michael E1; Wolfer, Terry A1 Coll Social Work, U South Carolina, Columbia sherr@gwm.sc.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61497212?accountid=14667

Abstract: The social work profession has a remarkable opportunity & challenge to reconnect with its religious roots & develop services in partnership with religious congregations. The professional literature, however, has provided social work educators with limited guidance in terms of resources & curriculum strategies for preparing students & practitioners for competent practice in religious settings. This paper posits the efficacy of a decision case module for covering content on Charitable Choice & working with religious congregations. The Grace House Ministry, a decision case written specifically for the curriculum module is included at the end. 1 Appendix, 44 References. Adapted from the source document.

Comments on "In Good Faith"

Author: [Unknown]

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61430094?accountid=14667

Abstract: Comments are offered on "In Good Faith," a decision case describing a dilemma faced by the Board of Directors of Food for All (FFA), a faith-based, nonprofit organization committed to ending global hunger. Carl Holland's ecosystems analysis emphasizes the impact of Touissant's limited English comprehension & his cultural background on his failure to successfully adjust to the training situation in Louisiana. In Mark Rodgers maintains that the FFA should have developed a contingency plan to deal with individuals who are trained in developed nations but fail to return to their own country to practice. Further, the FFA also needs to become more aware of social work practice from a multicultural perspective. Ian Bedford contends that two ethical issues are involved: (1) the structure of the international internship program that removes people from their own communities & relocates them for training in the US where they are exposed to a materialist culture, taught agricultural techniques in a foreign language, & expected to adapt to US customs for institutionalized community living; & (2) Touissant's health & well-being should the INS be notified, which might lead to his being imprisoned or forced to live underground. 7 References.

Reconciliation: A Paradoxical Idea and Ideal for Christian Social Work in the 21st Century

Author: Johnson, Timothy J11 Robert Wesleyan Coll, Rochester, NY johnsont@roberts.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61538730?accountid=14667

Abstract: Our calling to be committed to reconciliation as Christians is particularly paradoxical for Christian social workers. A common response to the complexities of our times is constructed realities that ignore truth coming out of that which is paradoxical. The genesis of modern social work, the nature of Christian reconciliation, & our relevance to issues at the threshold of the 21st century are a call for us to minister in places where truth seems to conflict with truth. 14 References. Adapted from the source document.

Reconceptualizing Faith and Its Relation to Community Ministry: A Model of Christian Faith Practices

Author: Hugen, Beryl1; Wolfer, Terry A; Garland, Diana R; Myers, Dennis; Sherwood, David A; Sheridan, Paula1 Calvin Coll, Grand Rapids, MI bhugen@calvin.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61508574?accountid=14667

Abstract: Increased attention is currently focused on the role of faith communities (congregations) in addressing community problems & persons in need. This attention raises questions about the relationship of faith & community ministry, specifically, how volunteerism may affect the life of faith of congregations & congregational members. This article offers conceptual definitions of faith & community ministry, explores a variety of psychological & theological measurements of faith, & begins to explore their relationship. A conceptual model of Christian faith practices, along with a newly constructed measurement scale (Practices of Christian Faith), is described. The scale was administered to a sample of congregational members (N = 7,403), nearly 50% of whom reported participating in community ministry, from 34 congregations. 2 Tables, 38 References. Adapted from the source document.

Social Workers' Countertransference Issues with Spiritually-Similar Clients

Author: Raines, James C11 Illinois State U, Normal jcraine@ilstu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61551565?accountid=14667

Abstract: This is a qualitative study of social workers' countertransference issues when providing psychotherapy for spiritually similar clients. It examined five pairs of social workers & their respective clients who shared the same faith. In-depth interviews were conducted with each member of the pair, covering four areas: spiritual similarities, assessment issues, transference & countertransference issues, & evaluation of progress. Three major themes appeared across participants: (1) a "halo effect" in which the client's pathology was underestimated by their social worker; (2) a "narcissism of minor differences" where the social workers overemphasized the differences between themselves & their clients; & (3) a denial of "sinful feelings," including anger & attraction toward their clients. Each of these themes is then discussed on two levels: spiritual & psychosocial. To overcome these problems, social workers are encouraged to explore & categorize their affective responses in order to help clients heal & grow. 3 Tables, 2 Figures, 74 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Social+Workers%27+Countertransference+Issues+with+Spiritually-Similar+Clients&amp;rft.au=Raines%2C+James+C&amp;rft.aulast=Raines&amp;rft.aufirst=James&amp;rft.date=2003-10-01&amp;rft.volume=30&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.spage=256&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Music as a Medium to the Spiritual Themes of Forgiveness, Service, and Social Justice in Addiction Counselor Training

Author: Gallant, Wilfred1; Holosko, Michael; Gallant, Melanie1 U Windsor, Ontario gallantw@uwindsor.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61556976?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper describes the use of Christian spiritually oriented music intervention & recovery themes in a workshop designed for addiction counselor training. It illustrates how specific content themes for training & education for counselors can be anchored in music that has been developed & field tested for use with addicted clients. No published accounts of using music intervention as the basis for psychoeducation training of addiction counselors are reported in the literature. The paper concludes with a summary of the relationship between spiritually oriented music themes, selected topics of recovery, & spiritual growth. Implications are directed toward clergy, pastoral counselors, social workers, addiction counselors, & music therapists in their own quest toward personal fulfillment & professional growth in the spirit. 1 Figure, 39 References. Adapted from the source document.

Author: Hugen, Beryl11 Calvin Coll, Grand Rapids, MI bhugen@calvin.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61502319?accountid=14667

Abstract: Recently political leaders & policymakers have focused attention on how to increase the presence & vitality of faith in supporting persons in need & in strengthening communities. This call to expand the role of faith in social welfare programming has resulted in an assortment of new understandings of the place of faith in social work practice. This essay explores some of the opportunities & challenges involved in this new configuration of faith for Christians in social work. 23 References. Adapted from the source document.

Integrating Spirituality and Religion into Direct Practice Assessment

Author: Winship, James P11 Social Work Dept, U Wisconsin, Whitewater winshipj@uww.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61519488?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article concerns one aspect of direct practice with individuals -- the use of assessment. It is written for Christians who are social workers in direct practice in a variety of settings. The article will examine the organizational contexts within which assessment takes place & discuss the implications of these for the appropriate integration of religion/spirituality. It will explain reasons why religion & spirituality are integral to effective holistic assessments, assessments that encompass the social environment, strengths, & clients' telling of their stories. The use of brief pencil & paper assessments & open-ended questions in initial assessments are discussed as ways that open the door to clients talking about religious/spiritual dimensions of their situations. Finally, assessment approaches that can be used after the client has disclosed spiritual/religious issues are discussed. For the Christian social worker, distinctions between working with other Christians & adherents to other faiths will be clarified. 1 Table, 71 References. Adapted from the source document.

Empowered Use of Self in Social Work: Understanding Personal Narrative through Guided Autobiography

Author: Seaton, Craig11 Human Services Program, Trinity Western U, Langley, British Columbia seaton@twu.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/60483389?accountid=14667

Abstract: Many social workers & other helping professionals leave their work disillusioned & frustrated. Sometimes this relates to organizational problems, or lack of resources to accomplish mandated objectives, but often the real difficulty is one that finds the worker feeling overwhelmed or "burned out." This article will argue that those in training for social work as well as those in practice can find a new sense of empowerment for service through a greater sense of self-understanding, contributing to a more effective use of the self. Knowing who we are as people is crucial, not for just some vague sense of self-actualization, but to allow us to identify with the common humanity we share with clients, & to allow us to utilize life experience (including formal education) more effectively. The "Guided Autobiography" method is presented as a useful procedure to enhance self-awareness. 55 References. Adapted from the source document.

Offering Hope in Romania: Efforts to Empower through Cross-Cultural Social Work

Author: Patterson, Elizabeth11 Veritas Sighisoara, Romania etatt@hotmail.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61509759?accountid=14667

Abstract: Throughout the world, there are people suffering with little hope for their future. Christian social workers have an opportunity to utilize their skills internationally in order to bring about hope through faith-based initiatives. This paper will present a model of empowerment offering spiritual, economic, & social hope by creating faith-based social assistance programs in Sighisoara, Romania. These programs are empowering local people as leaders of social, educational, & economic projects. The personal experiences of 3 Romanian staff members working with Veritas Sighisoara will be shared as a basis for discussion of opportunities, benefits, & challenges of involvement in international & cross-cultural social work practice. 11 References. Adapted from the source document.

Can Reverse Mission Internationalize Social Work -- Without the Hegemony of Religious, Social, or Intellectual Conversion?

Author: Abram, Faye Y1; Shufeldt, Gregory; Rose, Kelle1 School Social Service, Saint Louis U abramfy@slu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61452479?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article presents the concept & models of "reverse mission" as they come from the fields of theology & mission studies & describe experiential courses in international social work that are designed as adaptations of reverse mission for social work. The courses, one in Mexico & the other in Ghana, West Africa, integrate content about the global context of practice into the social work curriculum & sensitize students to the worldwide interconnections of oppression. Student projects provide evidence that these courses also facilitate the transfer of learning from developing countries into social work practice with oppressed &/or culturally diverse populations at home in the US. Exploration of the utility of reverse mission as an approach to international social work education & practice, however, raises questions about its suitability for social work & whether faith-based organizations &/or educational programs can appropriate it for the profession without the hegemony of religious, social, or intellectual conversion. 1 Figure, 49 References. Adapted from the source document.

Literacy and the Developing World

Author: Cross, Nick11 Monmouth U, Frankfort, OH nhcross@hotmail.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61452483?accountid=14667

Abstract: Education is one of the basic human rights of all people, but is denied to millions around the world. The reasons are many, including HIV/AIDS, poor sanitation, lack of money, lack of schools, lack of teachers, hunger, violence, & corruption. To combat these factors, citizens must be educated & obstacles must be eliminated. The best way to accomplish this is through holistic methods such as providing free meals to students who attend schools. This article demonstrates the importance of literacy, reviews statistics about literacy in Africa & worldwide, enumerates factors affecting education, & finally summarizes some of the literacy efforts internationally. 2 Appendixes, 28 References. Adapted from the source document.

Experiencing South Africa as an African American Woman: Moving from the Strengths Perspective to Racial and Gender "Victorization"

Author: Battle-Walters, Kimberly11 Azusa Pacific U, CA kwalters@apu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61490374?accountid=14667

Abstract: The year 2004 marks the 10th anniversary of the official end of apartheid & the beginning of a postapartheid, democratic South Africa. Front 1994 until 2004, South Africa made commendable strides in racial & gender reconciliation, & from a strengths perspective has built from past deficits & now provides a model for international reconciliation. This article, however, looks at moving beyond the strengths perspective & applying the theoretical concept of racial & gender "victorization" within a South African context. 4 References. Sustained Involvement Produces Results: Oklahoma Team Partners to Help Russian Orphans

Author: Endicott, Lanny11 Social Work Program, Oral Roberts U, Tulsa, OK lendicott@oru.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61510620?accountid=14667

Abstract: Is there evidence that work with Russian orphans has produced positive results? In seeking some answers, a survey was administered to orphan graduates during a winter leadership camp (Jan 2004) conducted by a team from Southern Hills Baptist Church (Tulsa) & additional OK participants. For the past several years, the team from OK has partnered with faith-based Children's HopeChest (Nadezhda Fund in Russia) to provide camps & ongoing support for older orphans. This article explores the results of the survey & their implications & discusses the team's expanded mission with Russian orphans. 1 Appendix. Adapted from the source document.

Soylent Green Is People: Cannibalism of the World's Women and Children through Sexual Trafficking and Prostitution

Author: Thompson, Lisa L11 Abolition Sexual Trafficking, Salvation Army National Headquarters, Alexandria, VA lisa_thompson@usn.salvationarmy.org

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61509752?accountid=14667

Abstract: Sexual trafficking of persons for purposes of commercial sexual exploitation (eg, prostitution, pornography, live-sex shows, & stripping) is rampant throughout the world. Most antitrafficking efforts focus on supply-side or "push" factors primarily attributable to trafficking victims, but typically ignore demand or "pull" factors such as the demand for commercial sex created by the existence, tolerance, & fostering of sex markets. As such, governments (with the exception of Sweden) & nongovernmental organizations alike have been largely unsuccessful in stemming the flow of vulnerable women & children into the global sex industry. The roots of the resistance to linking sexual trafficking with prostitution & to aggressively seeking the sex industry's extermination are deep, & often lie embedded in factors such as profit, corruption, myths about the "harmlessness" of prostitution, cultural attitudes about male sexual privilege, & gender inequality. To expose self-serving motives & the fallacy of "permission-giving" attitudes & beliefs, this article examines demand for commercial sex, specifically prostitution & trafficking for prostitution, metaphorically as cannibalism. The author has traveled to Bangladesh, the Netherlands, & Mexico, & reports some firsthand observations. 37 References. Adapted from the source document.

Coping with Difficult Life Transitions by Older Adults: The Role of Religion

Author: Van Hook, Mary P1; Rivera, Jose Oscar1 U Central Florida, Orlando

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61525834?accountid=14667

Abstract: Religion has been associated with improved well-being of older adults. An anonymous study was conducted in central FL with community-living older adults to identify how they coped with difficult life transitions. English & Spanish versions of the brief RCOPE scale developed by Pargament were used to identify how older adults coped & perceptions of helpfulness of religious coping. Older adults who participated described frequent use of positive religious coping strategies, especially "Asking forgiveness for sins," "Looking for a stronger connection with God," & "Seeking God's love & care." Somewhat less important were "Trying to put plans into action with God," "Trying to see how God could try to strengthen me," & "Focusing on religion to stop worrying about my problems." Negative strategies were seldom mentioned. Use of positive coping strategies was viewed as helpful. Women were more likely than men to use positive coping strategies & to view religion as helpful. Minimal racial/ethnic differences emerged. Findings support the value of helping older adults with a religious orientation in their lives to use religious support that enables them to feel that they have some control over their situation & are cared for by God as ways to cope with difficult life transitions. 3 Tables, 41 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Impact of Caring in Faith-Based Social Service Programs: What Participants Say

Author: Yancey, Gaynor I1; Atkinson, Kelly M1 BSW Program, School Social Work, Baylor U, Waco, TX

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61545141?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article is based on a qualitative study of 15 faith-based programs & their social service provision to the urban poor. Through the words of the participants, the following questions are discussed. What compelled participants to go to faith-based organizations to obtain social services? What were their experiences with these faith-based organizations? What impacted them in such a way that they chose to return, time & again, for the care that was provided by these faith-based organizations? The following reflections give voice to the simple & life changing, the challenging & mundane happenings that comprise participants' encounters with these faith-based programs. They felt cared for, treated as significant & valued persons. Adapted from the source document.

Religion, Spirituality, and Geographic Region in the USA: An Examination of Regional Similarities and Differences among Social Workers in Direct Practice

Author: Furman, Leola Dyrud1; Benson, Perry W; Canda, Edward R1 U North Dakota, Grand Forks furmanlfurman@aol.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61540222?accountid=14667

Abstract: Based on a 1997 random study stratified by US census divisions to determine how social workers across the nation approach the topics of religion & spirituality in direct practice & education, this article explores regional similarities & differences regarding the appropriateness of religion & spirituality in relation to a wide range of practice issues, the use & appropriateness of religious & spiritually-based interventions, & the relationship between religion & spirituality, & education & professional ethics. Social workers' individual religious & spiritual practices are also examined. Social workers in the South & Midwest were more likely to endorse the integration of religion & spirituality in direct practice & education, sometimes significantly so, than their peers in the North & West. Both regionally & as a whole, however, the social workers in this study tended to be homogenous in their responses. The authors speculate that the accreditation guidelines & policies of the Council on Social Work Education may have an impact on this finding. As the only national study of social workers' attitudes on religion & spirituality in direct practice & education, the findings can be used for comparative & historical purposes in future regional & national studies on this subject. 3 Tables, 22 References. Adapted from the source document.

A Personal Reflection on Long Distance Secondary Caregiving: Spirituality as a Strength

Author: Collins, Wanda L11 Raymond A. Kent School Social Work, U Louisville, KY wanda.collins@louisville.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61555397?accountid=14667

Abstract: Caring for an aging parent can be a stressful experience, especially from a distance. While long distance relationships are not always easy to manage, they can become taxing & demanding when they involve caring for aging parents who live more than a few miles away. Caregiving from a distance is of particular concern when adult children are also handling their own demands with work & family. This article describes the personal experiences of an African American social work practitioner providing long distance care as a secondary caregiver. Challenges are included & blessings related to secondary caregiving for a loved one diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease are discussed. The article concludes with spiritual self-care practices that facilitate the reduction of caregiving stress. 5 References. Adapted from the source document.

For the Glory of God: How Monotheism Led to Reformations, Science, Witch-Hunts, and the End of Slavery

Author: Hodge, David R; Hodge, David R

A Skinny White Christian Moves to the Ghetto

Author: Roseberry, Phillip

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61362986?accountid=14667

Abstract: Reprinted in commemoration of the 30th anniversary of his death (murdered by an unknown assailant in 1975), this essay offers the author's personal reflections on Christian social work practice in the ghetto in Brooklyn (New York City) in the early 1970s. Roseberry's experiences with the Shiloh program, which allowed him to integrate his religious beliefs with practice in working with minority children & their families, are recounted, along with his observations on how this work challenged his faith. K. Hyatt Stewart

The Journey to Resiliency: An Integrative Framework for Treatment for Victims and Survivors of Family Violence

Author: Cairns-Descoteaux, Bonita11 Roberts Wesleyan Coll, Rochester, NY Descoteauxb@roberts.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61367941?accountid=14667

Abstract: The model presented in this article provides a framework for working with victims of family violence that differs from other integrative frameworks in the professional literature by including a spiritual dimension. There are four parts to the model that are discussed in this paper: development of a healthy self-concept, connection to a loving God, healing of memories, & consolidating personal growth. The article begins with a process to assess clients, based on their journey towards resiliency. Using this assessment as a basis for intervention, the social worker can begin working with the client, using the four phases in this model. References. Adapted from the source document.

Spiritual Change in Treatment of Substance Dependence

Author: Stewart, Chris1; Koeske, Gary F1 School Social Work, U Pittsburgh, PA

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61347333?accountid=14667

Abstract: Research investigating the role of spirituality is still in the exploratory stage. There is even less empirical work done with spirituality in the drug & alcohol treatment arena. This research sought to explore clients' spirituality change during the course of substance abuse treatment. A sample of 70 clients from two different programs was tested before & after treatment to determine any change in spirituality. There was evidence of significant change in several dimensions of spirituality. Implications for social work practice are discussed. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document.

Informal vs. Formal Support Networks among the Elderly and the Role of Religion

Author: Sherr, Michael E1; Shields, Glenn; King, William; Curran, Mary A1 Northwestern Nazarene U, Nampa, ID mesherr@nnu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61347370?accountid=14667

Abstract: This study was conducted to examine how older adults use informal & formal support systems based in a predominantly rural community. A random sample (n=262) of older adults was surveyed from a county in Northwestern Ohio. In addition, the role of religiosity was explored as a factor in utilization of support systems. Data from this study indicated that older adults have & use extensive informal support networks to maintain & enhance their social functioning. Religiosity was found to significantly influence the extent & use of informal support networks. However, it was not found to be a significant influence in formal support networks. The findings suggest implications for social work practitioners, church leaders, & religious laypeople. Recommendations for future research also are discussed. References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Informal+vs.+Formal+Support+Networks+among+the+Elderly+and+the+Role+of+Religion&amp;rft.au=Sherr%2C+Michael+E%3BShields%2C+Glenn%3BKing%2C+William%3BCurran%2C+Mary+A&amp;rft.aulast=Sherr&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft.date=2005-01-01&amp;rft.volume=32&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.spage=341&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

A Christian Perspective on Poverty and Social Justice: Sin Is More Than Just Flawed Character

Author: Gauer, Laura

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61372522?accountid=14667

Abstract: In the profession of social work, the word "sin" is often tied to the moral model which views personal deficit as the explanation for social ills. This theological terminology may cause some to believe there is a fundamental rift between Christianity & social work. Additionally, many Christians still hold some of these beliefs, failing to recognize structural inequalities & the biblical mandate for social justice. This paper looks at the religious roots of social work as well as the historical split between fundamental & liberal Christianity. It is intended to provide insight for the social work community as well as a scriptural view of social justice for Christians in the helping profession. References. Adapted from the source document.

Clarifying the Distinctions and Connections between Spirituality and Religion

Author: Hodge, David R1; McGrew, Charlene Chen1 Program Research Religion & Urban Civil Society, U Pennsylvania, Philadelphia dhodge@sas.upenn.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61512815?accountid=14667

Abstract: Based on a survey of a nationally representative sample of NACSW affiliated social work students in publicly funded gradate programs (N = 88), this study explores students' perceptions of the connections & distinctions between spirituality & religion. More specifically, using qualitative analysis of the content of interviews, we explore how spirituality & religion are defined & what, if any, relationship exists between these two constructs in the eyes of respondents. The results indicated that students define spirituality primarily in terms of belief in or connection with God or a Higher power. In contrast to spirituality, multiple themes were often used to define religion, with the most prominent being the practice of one's spirituality or faith. The vast majority of respondents reported that some type of relationship exists between spirituality & religion, with only 4% stating that no relationship exists between the two concepts. 4 Tables, 43 References. Adapted from the source document.

Before -- and after -- the Political Rhetoric: Faith-Based Child and Family Welfare Services

Author: Garland, Diana R1; Chamiec-Case, Rick1 School Social Work, Baylor U, Waco, TX

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61563034?accountid=14667

Abstract: Long before the term "faith-based" became a term of political debate & platform, religious groups were actively sponsoring programs of child & family welfare services through agencies that provided residential & foster care, adoption services, crisis care, & family preservation services -- in short, the whole range of child & family welfare services. These agencies continue to play significant roles in many communities & their social services. This article explores the distinctive contribution these agencies are making, key areas for research, the development of models of service, & the potential of these agencies to become significant partners to public agencies & funders (private & public) in the provision of services for the most vulnerable children & families in our society. 20 References. Adapted from the source document.

African American Faith-Based Organizations' Involvement in the Adoption of African American Children: Are They the Missing Link?

Author: Morris, Betty1; Rambo-Freeman, Debra; Powell, Cheryl1 Valdosta State U, GA bmorris@valdosta.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61527620?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article describes the participation of African American faith-based organizations (FBOs) in the adoption of African American children. It asserts that a close look at policies & procedures that impede successful adoption of African American children is required. Practitioners who lack respect for African American families & the black community weaken efforts to create problem solving strategies to aid African American children within the child welfare system. Collaboration with African American (FBOs) is a good starting point for practitioners to increase their knowledge & understanding of culturally & ethnically diverse groups. 39 References. Adapted from the source document.
