The Praxis of Social Work: A Model of How Faith Informs Practice Informs Faith

Author: Singletary, Jon E11 School Social Work, Baylor U, Waco, TX Jon_Singletary@baylor.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61537087?accountid=14667

Abstract: Based on liberation theology's approach to thought & action, this article presents the praxis of social work & offers a model for critical reflection upon committed action. The discussion reviews the usage of praxis in liberation theology & social work literature. The proposed model begins with the existing committed practice of social workers, moves to reflection on social work practice (including a consideration of the role of religion &/or spirituality), & aims for an improved, more intelligent, more reflective & more informed practice of social work. The model is a circular process for how thought & action, particularly theological reflection & social work practice, might inform each other in the life of a social worker who seeks to incorporate the role of faith, religion, &/or spirituality in her or his practice. 3 Figures, 37 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=The+Praxis+of+Social+Work%3A+A+Model+of+How+Faith+Informs+Practice+Informs+Faith&amp;rft.au=Singletary%2C+Jon+E&amp;rft.aulast=Singletary&amp;rft.aufirst=Jon&amp;rft.date=2005-04-01&amp;rft.volume=32&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.spage=56&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Volunteerism, Social Work, and the Church: A Historic Overview and Look into the Future

Author: Sherr, Michael E1; Straughan, Hope Haslam1 Dept Social Work, Northwest Nazarene U, Nampa, ID mesherr@nnu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61373814?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article sets forth the relationship between volunteerism, social work, & the church as consisting of three discernable periods: 1) recognition of volunteerism, rooted in the church, as the origin of the profession; 2) a general attempt by the social work profession to separate & distinguish itself from volunteerism & religiously motivated services; & 3) periods of reconciliation in times of need. The authors conclude by positing the need for context-specific optimal partnerships between religious & secular professionals & religious & secular volunteers. Two case examples of context-specific optimal partnerships will be followed by implications for social work educators & practitioners. 59 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Volunteerism%2C+Social+Work%2C+and+the+Church%3A+A+Historic+Overview+and+Look+into+the+Future&amp;rft.au=Sherr%2C+Michael+E%3BStraughan%2C+Hope+Haslam&amp;rft.aulast=Sherr&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft.date=2005-07-01&amp;rft.volume=32&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=97&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Volunteerism%2C+Social+Work%2C+and+the+Church%3A+A+Historic+Overview+and+Look+into+the+Future&amp;rft.au=Sherr%2C+Michael+E%3BStraughan%2C+Hope+Haslam&amp;rft.aulast=Sherr&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft.date=2005-07-01&amp;rft.volume=32&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=97&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Social+Work+%26+Christianity&amp;rft.issn=07375778&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Volunteer Crisis Hotline Counselors: An Expression of Spirituality

Author: Praetorius, Regina Trudy1; Machtmes, Krisanna1 Louisiana State U, Baton Rouge

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61368641?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article addresses an often overlooked motivation for volunteers -- spirituality. The authors were asked to conduct a qualitative research study of motivations & benefits of volunteering at the local crisis center's 24-hour crisis hotline. The main questions asked of study participants were, what was the initial motivation for volunteering, & what keeps them coming back. Among the findings was a spiritual aspect to these volunteers' motivation to "work the lines." This spiritual motivation took on a variety of forms, including a desire to "give back" (altruism), a new perspective of one's own life & perceived challenges & obstacles, deeper understanding of the human condition, & realizing the interconnectedness among us all as part of the social fabric. The article shares these findings more in depth using the volunteers' own words. 22 References. Adapted from the source document.

Exploring the Influence of Religion among the Most Active Volunteer Service Club Members

Author: Sherr, Michael E1; Shields, Glenn1 Dept Social Work, Northwest Nazarene U, Nampa, ID mesherr@nnu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61347855?accountid=14667

Abstract: Using an in-depth case study method, this study explored how similar language, symbols, & rituals foster a cultural expectation for helping othSHers that is congruent to both religious & volunteer service club settings. Emerging themes suggest that religious beliefs & a sense of patriotism influenced the participation of the most active volunteer members in two service clubs from the Southeast & Midwest. Implications include supSHport for Cnaan's "club theory" as well as a reminder for social workers to take note of & appreciate the influences that may stir the most active volunteers to participate in community service. 55 References. Adapted from the source document.

She Saw Something in Me: Relationships between Faith Community Volunteers and Mothers Leaving Welfare

Author: Williamson, Sabrina11 School Social Work, Indiana U, Bloomington sabawill@indiana.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61373174?accountid=14667

Abstract: The PRWORA legislation of 1996 encourages partnerships between faithSHbased groups & government agencies in providing services to families leaving welfare for work. Although this has led to an increase in the provision of faith-based social services, there is a lack of published research concerning best practices. The findings presented in this article concern the training & support that congregation members need in order to work successfully with families in this population. The article concludes with a discussion of implications for future practice & research. 25 References. Adapted from the source document.

Methodological Challenges in Identifying Promising and Exemplary Practices in Urban Faith-Based Social Service Programs

Author: Rogers, Robin K1; Yancey, Gaynor; Singletary, Jon1 School Social Work, Baylor U, Waco, TX

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61371528?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper considers the historical context in which current research on faith-based organizations (FBOs) is occurring & is followed by our discussion of a multi-year, multi-site study funded by The Pew Charitable Trusts to study promising & exemplary practices in social service programs operated by congregations & faith-based non profits. We focus on the challenges we have faced which include determining the unit of analysis, defining the types of programs to be studied, recognizing the diverse use of language in operationalizing terms, & managing the complexity of an interdisciplinary research team. Implications relate to the importance of recognizing the historical context & its accompanying political & definitional concerns; selecting units of analysis; ways to build teams across multiple sites & disciplines; & the political nature of "best practice" approaches. While the paper focuses on the research process, there are practice implications for organizations in this era of faith-based initiatives. 33 References. Adapted from the source document.

Sustainable Development of International Social Work Practice in the Christian Community

Author: Rodgers, Mark E11 Dominican U Graduate School Social Work, River Forest, IL mrodgers@dom.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61384340?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article focuses on the concept of sustainable development for Christian social workers involved in the field of international social work. After a brief historical review of the nature & context of international social work, it explores the expanded roles of governmental & non-governmental organizations in providing global social work services, with an emphasis on the United Nations World Summit for Social Development & the Millennium Development Goals. It then details the relief & development practices of large-scale, faith-based organizations such as the Salvation Army, World Vision, Christian Children's Fund, Bread for the World, & Catholic Social Services. The reality of global social work practice includes such diverse areas as poverty, the role of women, human rights violations, bio-diversity challenges, the HIV/AIDS pandemic, human trafficking, children & maternal health crises, lack of education, child & adult mortality, & war & terrorism. Finally, it discusses the concept of sustainable development with an emphasis on education for international practice as well as the use of Christian social workers as a force for positive change in the world. It challenges Christian social workers, as well as professional organizations like NACSW, to review their global awareness; to assess their cultural competencies; to expand their awareness of the history, values, ethics, & language of the nation in which they will practice; & to expand their knowledge of local culture, religion, & current professional activities while at the same time avoiding the emphasis on economic development over social development known as disjointed development as well as social work practice reflecting professional imperialism. Adapted from the source document.

Social Justice and Social Services within the Catholic Church

Author: Himchak, Maureen V11 Kean U, Union, NJ mvhimchak@aol.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61366577?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article explores the human rights perspective in social work, social teachings & social services in the Catholic Church. It explains the human rights perspective as emerging from its origin of three Christian beliefs: the Trinity, the Incarnation & the Mystical Body of Christ. Seven major themes of the social justice teachings of the Catholic Church are compared with the Tropman's model of the attitudes & dimensions of social justice. These social justice themes & attitudes are represented in all cultures through the ministry of the Catholic Church's largest social service organization, Caritas Internationalis. 1 Table, 15 References. Adapted from the source document.

Best Interest of a Child: An Introduction to International Adoptions

Author: Price, Karin11 Dillon International, Inc., Richmond, IN Karin@dillonadopt.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61370875?accountid=14667

Abstract: The plight of children growing up without parents is an increasing global crisis. In this article, the author presents international adoption as one solution for parentless children. Her personal stories provide insights to the issues confronting those who work in the field of international adoption. A description of Dillon International's efforts to provide a plan in the best interest of each child also addresses the challenge. 24 References. Adapted from the source document.

Embracing Spirituality as an Element of Professional Self-Care

Author: Collins, Wanda Lott11 Raymond A. Kent School Social Work, U Louisville, KY wanda.collins@louisville.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61361627?accountid=14667

Abstract: Fatigue, overextension with professional commitments, & exposure to human cruelty may expose social work practitioners to high levels of stress. While the goal is not to eliminate stress, social workers can explore possibilities for its management. Turning to religion or spirituality is a potential coping mechanism that can help social workers create harmony in their personal lives to lessen burnout & day-to-day emotional stress in their professional roles. This paper examines spiritual self-care practices that social work practitioners can incorporate as a line of defense against distress & to minimize the deleterious effects of stress. 30 References. Adapted from the source document.
